```
On 5/20/2012 8:31 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Yesterday I was looking at some COBOL code (not mine) which did not
> transform correctly.
…[92 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

As you have pointed out, it is perfectly valid COBOL to have a read 
statement without any explicit scope termination.  The COBOL compiler 
understands it and generates the expected behavior.

If it were me, I would want to change the transformation tool to detect 
the implied termination of the READ statement.  I would think the 
presence of any COBOL imperative verb after the READ without an 
intervening full stop, AT END, NOT AT END, INVALID KEY, or END-READ 
should result in an implied END-READ just before the imperative verb. 
In your example, the ADD statement implies that the previous READ 
statement is complete.

Obviously I cannot know how difficult it would be to modify the tool. 
But I still think the best long-term solution would be to fix it to make 
it as robust as possible.  That's what I would want if I were a 
customer.  The users shouldn't have to change the COBOL source code to 
make it compatible with the tool.  That adds more risk, but it's 
probably the best you can do as a short-term fix.

If the input COBOL source program does not compile successfully, I don't 
believe the transformation tool can be faulted if it does not produce a 
working output program.

DocDwarf pointed out that it's not a good idea to ADD 1 TO IN-COUNT 
before verifying that a record was successfully read.  So I don't think 
it's very good COBOL code even if it is ISO standard COBOL.

Would this problem still exist if the original code looked more like this?

READ INFILE
IF INFILE-STATUS NOT = "00"
     (do some error-handling)
ELSE
     ADD 1 TO IN-COUNT
END-IF

With kindest regards,
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-22T04:03:54+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a1v7bdF9dnU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <N_adnZLwaoMWuSfSnZ2dnUVZ5vOdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

```
Arnold Trembley wrote:
> On 5/20/2012 8:31 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
<snipped previous>
>
> If it were me, I would want to change the transformation tool to
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> statement is complete.

Yes, my initial thought was to do just that. The COBOL parsing does have a 
reserved word list and it can identify them, but it isn't quite that simple.

What happens currently is that when an IO verb is identified  the Toolset 
pushes the appropriate scope delimiter onto a stack, then reads the context 
of the verb looking for that delimiter or a full stop. (there is a "sanity 
check" to ensure it doesn't read too far and if it exceeds this number of 
lines without a delimiter, it terminates the processing for that verb and 
flags it in the source.) This deals with nested verbs very well (provided 
they are scoped); new verbs encountered in the context have THEIR delimiter 
pushed on the stack and when a delimiter is encountered in the source the 
corresponding one is popped off the stack until the stack is empty or the 
sanity check is exceeded.

There is more to it than that because checks have to be made for all the 
VERBS we are interested in, not just READ, and the various possible phrases 
that are part of a verb have to be parsed, recognised, and processed.

The lines of source which encompass the scope are passed back to the main 
process as an array of lines and they are then used to generate DAL invokes.

Scope is pretty critical to the whole process and it works when scope is 
observed. (It actually works OK even when there are missing scope delimiters 
but in this particular case it skipped some lines. I could alter the sanity 
check for READ to fix it but that isn't a good solution.)

Of course, if scope is not being observed and there are conditional 
statements coded  (IF, EVALUATE...) then all Hell breaks loose so it is a 
good idea for programmers to observe scope even where they don't have to (as 
in this case).


>
> Obviously I cannot know how difficult it would be to modify the tool.

It is "non-trivial"... :-)

I considered posting some of the code here but decided against it. (It is C# 
and although I think most people here could follow it, it does use a lot of 
Regular Expressions and the constructions are unfamilair to people. However, 
the whole process (it is ONE Method within MOSTAMD that sorts out the COBOL 
Verbs) is only around 100 lines so it wouldn't be impossible to rewrite, 
using a different approach to deal with scope.)

I think it is something I will put into the list of TODOs... but at low 
priority; we have an easy workaround  by fixing the exisitng Legacy 
source...


> But I still think the best long-term solution would be to fix it to
> make it as robust as possible.

I agree. It is as robust as I can make it currently and the Toolset has 
never hung or crashed in production. But that doesn't mean that approaches 
cannot be improved. I recently spent a few days writing better optimization 
into the generated databases... They were fine before, but now they are 
better :-) I think the scope problem falls in that category. The Toolset 
detects and handles over 4000 possible conditions; it has been very 
carefully designed to provide meaningful error messages and assist with 
diagnosis.


> That's what I would want if I were a
> customer.  The users shouldn't have to change the COBOL source code to
> make it compatible with the tool.

I agree. And mostly they don't. Millions of lines of COBOL have been 
processed by the toolset without anything having to be changed. But things 
have to be considered on balance. Improving the legacy is not a bad thing to 
do even if you weren't using the PRIMA toolset.

There is an argument that says that as long as you are running and 
maintaining code you should keep it as good as you can.

Getting Source Sets (COPY books) right, removing redundant OCCURS clauses, 
reviewing messy constructs are all things that help to ease the maintenance 
burden, never mind any future conversion/migration.

> That adds more risk, but it's
> probably the best you can do as a short-term fix.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> produce a working output program.

Yes, I think that is fair too. However, the program to hand DOES compile 
(and execute) correctly so it really is a toolset problem. The fix is easy 
but it does mean a pre-edit of the existing source.

>
> DocDwarf pointed out that it's not a good idea to ADD 1 TO IN-COUNT
> before verifying that a record was successfully read.  So I don't
> think it's very good COBOL code even if it is ISO standard COBOL.

I have come to the point of thinking there isn't ANY good COBOL out there... 
:-) (Except what I write myself, of course... I'm pretty sure that most 
programmers feel that way, whether they admit it or not... :-))

Occasionally I come across something which makes me think the person who 
wrote it knew what they were doing, but there is a lot of stuff that evokes 
the opposite reaction, too.

The brutal reality is that if code works, it is "acceptable".

There simply isn't time or money to finesse it up after it is working. 
"There's always time to do it over, but there's never time to do it right."

As programmers gain experience they write "better code" in the sense that 
they can get something working quicker than they could when they started...

>
> Would this problem still exist if the original code looked more like
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> END-IF

Yes. The problem is with the READ scope. (If they use AT END/INVALID KEY 
they HAVE to scope delimit it, otherwise the logic is wrong.)

It's really a design flaw in the Toolset because I didn't consider the use 
of File Status thoroughly. I catered for it at the SQLCODE level but never 
thought about the effect of no scope...

Thanks for your post Arnold. Good points and I think I need to consider 
changing the Toolset.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2012-05-22T01:01:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jpeogu$lsh$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <N_adnZLwaoMWuSfSnZ2dnUVZ5vOdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

```
In article <N_adnZLwaoMWuSfSnZ2dnUVZ5vOdnZ2d@giganews.com>,
Arnold Trembley  <arnold.trembley@att.net> wrote:

[snip]

>DocDwarf pointed out that it's not a good idea to ADD 1 TO IN-COUNT 
>before verifying that a record was successfully read.  So I don't think 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>END-IF

Actually, Mr Trembley, I was musing over this as I went through this 
thread and it finally coalesced - after more than a few decades - into my 
prejudice for a particular style.

Given that - and this is no longer the case - processing starts at the top 
of a file and grinds straight through to the end (and AT END, if I recall 
my PMAPs correctly, is a check for a stated file's status) then to 
predicate processing on NOT AT END is to predicate processing on the 
exception.

I hope I stated that clearly.  To rephrase... if the first READ is good 
then the odds are pretty decent that each successive READ will be good and 
that one will encounter FILE STATUS '10' only once.  That is the efficient 
cause behind (and folks can probably sing along to this):

    PERFORM 0000-HOUSEKEEPING THRU 0000-EXIT.
    PERFORM 5000-MAIN-LINE    THRU 5000-EXIT
        UNTIL NO-MORE-INPUT.
    PERFORM 9000-EOJ          THRU 9000-EXIT.
    GOBACK.

...

5000-MAIN-LINE.
    PERFORM 6000-READ-INFILE THRU 6000-EXIT.
    IF NO-MORE-INPUT
        GO TO 5000-EXIT.
...

5000-EXIT.
    EXIT.

... and if anything goes wrong in 0000-HOUSEKEEPING or 6000-READ-INFILE 
one sets NO-MORE-INPUT to true and in 9000-EOJ one checks INREC-CTR for a 
value of 0 and ABENDs everything.

If all goes well (and after a few 3:am calls things, for the most part, 
things would) then the only 'waste' is the check of NO-MORE-INPUT after 
each driver-file READ.  Reading an assembley-language listing of the code 
would then allow the programmer to code flags in fashions would would use 
a minimal amount of system resources.

Some have argued that this amounts to coding for micro-efficiency that 
modern hardware does not require.  Others have pointed out that the 
efficiency takes place behind the curtains (the classicists may muse over 
the derivation of 'obscene') of WORKING-STORAGE.

I'd better stop this before I point out how Napoleon was so rich his 
tableware was better-than-gold.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>
- **Date:** 2012-05-21T23:53:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<yJednYvwr4tUhibSnZ2dnUVZ5sydnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<jpeogu$lsh$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <N_adnZLwaoMWuSfSnZ2dnUVZ5vOdnZ2d@giganews.com> <jpeogu$lsh$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On 5/21/2012 8:01 PM, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article<N_adnZLwaoMWuSfSnZ2dnUVZ5vOdnZ2d@giganews.com>,
> Arnold Trembley<arnold.trembley@att.net>  wrote:
…[67 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Nowadays, Doc, I am more likely to code something like this:

PERFORM 1000-INITIALIZE-THE-PROGRAM
PERFORM 2000-PROCESS-TRANSACTIONS
     UNTIL 88-NO-MORE-RECORDS
PERFORM 3000-TERMINATE-PROGRAM
GOBACK.

2000-PROCESS-TRANSACTIONS.
     READ INPUT-FILE INTO WS-INPUT-RECORD
     AT END
         SET 88-NO-MORE-RECORDS TO TRUE
     NOT AT END
         ADD +1 TO TOTAL-INPUT-RECORDS
         PERFORM 2100-EDIT-1-TRANSACTION
     END-READ
     .

And we can still Abend the whole program if TOTAL-INPUT-RECORDS is zero, 
and there is only one READ statement for INPUT-FILE.

The lack of PERFORM THRU and GO TO EXIT allows the Enterprise COBOL for 
z/OS optimizer to work better.  At least, that's what they tell us and I 
have no evidence to doubt it.
```

Compilers might have changed since 1974?  That's possible, and advantage 
should be taken of it... a compile with the PMAP... errrrr, SOURCE option 
would generate the ASM to prove or disprove it.  What I notice in your 
example is that the READ is coded as part of the equivalent of MAIN-LINE; 
I've seen this style before and the argument against it assumed in the 
example I gave is that changes to the input file (or the input-file READ 
routine, eg using a similar skeleton for sequential processing of the main 
file to random processing of a KSDS, are purported to be more readily 
implementable when IO is isolated into its own paragraphs.

There are, of course, Very Good Reasons given for The Way Things Are Done 
Here in most shops I've worked in.

DD

> Yesterday I was looking at some COBOL code (not mine) which did not

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> think. 

I don't think this is quite as complicated as you seem to. 

>Other verbs, including IO verbs, could be nested in the INVALID 
> KEY/AT END branches; 

Yes, they could, but they must be preceded by either INVALID KEY or [NOT] AT END. If 
they are not, i.e., if *any verb at all* is encountered before one of these phrases, the READ 
scope *must* terminate immediately at that point.
```

On Tue, 22 May 2012 04:10:24 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Howard Brazee wrote:
>> The only times I've had problems with file statuses is when the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>So you use it all the time?

When checking for a successful OPEN for IBM VSAM (random access) files
'97' should be treated the same as '00'.  '97' means that the file in
question had not been closed successfully after a previous OPEN for
OUTPUT/I-O but that a successful verify had been done so the file is
safe to use.  While I never have specified or used them there is an
additional field that can be specified for the full VSAM return code
(8 bytes if I recall correctly).  If you ever get a client that uses
the field, you may want to flag the programs.  

Clark Morris    
>
>Pete.
```

    05  LIT-A  PIC X  VALUE 'A'.
    05  LIT-B  PIC X  VALUE 'B'.

MOVE LIT-A       TO  WS-SOME-FLAG
MOVE 'NO'        TO  WK-OTHER-FLG

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-21T19:54:33-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<533bc085-94b8-4440-9374-01c04e4d8569@pr3g2000pbb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <c0ikr7133uddpj412nhfsse029e8cio180@4ax.com> <jpel1r$ee3$1@reader1.panix.com> <a204rlFuqU1@mid.individual.net> <jpeov4$lsh$2@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On May 22, 1:08 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
> In article <a204rlFu...@mid.individual.net>,
>
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> ... and others not suitable for mention.

More 60s dogma, or possibly 70s. The earliest compilers that I used
would not share literals and eachy time one used "Y" it would create
another entry in the literal table. Creating 03 WS-Yes  PIC X VALUE
"Y". and using MOVE WS-Yes TO .. would save many bytes.

Later, the compilers would share literal entries so that no matter how
many MOVE "Y" TO .. were used there was only one byte consumed.
```

> core should be labelled 'dogma' instead of 'sound business practise'.

It wasn't dogma 'in the days', but its continued usage in these days
of better compilers is dogma.

> (Personally I saw it as a logical-but-absurd conclusion of 'Thou Shalt Not
> Hardcode (Except, Maybe, For Error-Messages)'.)
```

#### ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2012-05-21T10:44:15-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ankkr7p0s4c9hd76gt8d757nsgiqghh546@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Mon, 21 May 2012 13:31:22 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Yesterday I was looking at some COBOL code (not mine) which did not 
>transform correctly.
…[92 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.

It has been an awful long time since I've used native COBOL reads of
VSAM or Sequential files.  This is because many, many moons ago when
COBOL was not so robust and/or swift, we made all of our file reads
subroutine calls with copybooks that told it what to do and what the
return code was.  These were written in IBM Assembler.  True COBOL has
been much improved in this area over the years but since our software
must support MVS as well as VSE and I/O is where the biggest
difference lies, we've left this in place.

Whenever I do new development programs I most generally use
File-Status.  I prefer this over the AT END or INVALID KEY clauses
only because you can check for more precise conditions.  Personal
preference only and if I were working with simple sequential files,
I'd use the scope delimiters, but VSAM I'd use the FILE-STATUS no
matter which access method I was employing.

Regards,
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

SkippyPB wrote:
> On Mon, 21 May 2012 13:31:22 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
<snipped>
>
> It has been an awful long time since I've used native COBOL reads of
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Regards,

Thanks Steve, some useful observations.

It is important to note that using File Status doesn't mean that scope 
delimiters CAN'T be used on the READ; the problem is that it doesn't REQUIRE 
them to be used, whereas AT END/INVALID KEY does require them to be used.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <ankkr7p0s4c9hd76gt8d757nsgiqghh546@4ax.com> <a1v82nFet2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Tue, 22 May 2012 04:16:21 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Mon, 21 May 2012 13:31:22 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>them to be used, whereas AT END/INVALID KEY does require them to be used.

As I understand it, unless a verb is conditional scope delimiters
can't be used.  Thus a READ followed by an IF statement is not
conditional and therefore can not have an END-READ associated with it.
I suspect that this is redundant given an earlier posting of mine.

Clark Morris
>
>Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-22T11:29:24+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a201enFc1iU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <ankkr7p0s4c9hd76gt8d757nsgiqghh546@4ax.com> <a1v82nFet2U1@mid.individual.net> <317lr7pckljte2lloggqiogfp127dt17es@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Tue, 22 May 2012 04:16:21 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> I suspect that this is redundant given an earlier posting of mine.

No, I think I was in error for thinking that you COULD use a scope delimiter 
if it was an imperative form of the verb. Richard pointed it out in his post 
too.

I need to run some tests and I simply haven't had time...

Given that this is actually the case and borne out in practice, it gives me 
more incentive to look at changing the Toolset.

I may have based the design on a flawed understanding; I always thought that 
scope delimiters COULD be used, even with imperatives.

Probably the best strategy is to recognise scope termination by the next 
imperative verb being found. Currently, if a reserved word is found that is 
not part of the construct, and there is no INVALID KEY/AT END in force, then 
the current scope is considered to be terminated. However there are over 700 
reserved words in the list and I would now need to isolate and check for the 
ones that represent imperative statements... all work. :-(

Pete.
```

I ran the tests and found that my understanding was correct and it is NOT 
invalid to scope delimit an imperative READ statement. (At least, it isn't 
in Fujitsu NetCOBOL , which is what gets mostly used with the Toolset.) 
Please see "Test Results" topic in this same thread, responding to Richard.

>
> Given that this is actually the case and borne out in practice, it
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
While I haven't tested every verb, I am encouraged by the tests I DID run to 
believe that this understanding is correct.


> Probably the best strategy is to recognise scope termination by the
> next imperative verb being found. Currently, if a reserved word is
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> imperative statements... all work. :-(

Given that I can correct the problem fairly easily by simply scope 
terminating the READs, and given that this process can be automated easily 
to amend the Legacy codebase, the changes to the Toolset will now get pushed 
onto a low priority stack.

Pete.

When in doubt, consult the manual.  And the IBM Cobol Reference
copyright 2000, says this about End-Read:

This explicit scope terminator serves to delimit the scope of the READ
statement. END-READ permits a conditional READ statement to be nested
in another conditional statement. END-READ can also be used with an
imperative READ statement.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Conclusions WAS: Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-25T12:43:27+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a282thFldjU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <a281koFdh2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:

Sorry about blank post, finger trouble.

Thanks to all who participated in this thread. I gained some good insights 
and all of the posts have been considered before formulating action.

CONCLUSIONS:

1.  File Status is used more frequently that I had thought based on my 
experience. However, at this stage I believe the Toolset handling of it is 
adequate. (See ACTIONS, below)
2. When people use it they often tend to omit scope delimiters on READ. (If 
they use INVALID KEY/AT END they have no choice but to use a 
terminator/delimiter.)
3. It is NOT invalid to use scope delimiters on any verb that supports them, 
however, sometimes people don't.

I think they should be encouraged to use them, just for "tidiness", if 
nothing else.

ACTIONS:

1. The Toolset will be enhanced to strengthen the rudimentary checking of 
missing scope delimiters which is already present. (There are around 70 
imperative COBOL verbs which are a subset of the 610 reserved words I have 
already identified. These will be set into a separate Collection and used 
for detecting missing scope delimiters.)

2. The use of File Status is currently handled adequately and needs no 
update. There is a recognised problem that if the File Status field in the 
legacy code is uninitialized (no VALUE clause), it may not be set correctly 
in Werewolf code. Some programmers take the option to let OPEN initialize 
it; because OPENs are commented out in Werewolf code, it won't get the 
expected zero and processing may terminate. It would be possible for the 
Toolset to set a value of zero on it in the Transformed code, but at this 
stage I don't think it is worth the effort.

Again, thanks to all,

Pete.
```

I don't think I ever initialized file status since the OPEN statement
would by definition place a value in it.  If your tool in commenting
out the OPEN doesn't emulate this action the results will be
unpredictable unless the tool also comments out the file status check
after the OPEN.  This probably is not a problem for the other I-O
since any I-O operation will update the file status field and I
believe that is what your tool is doing  As someone who used file
status only on VSAM files (which include sequential or ESDS files and
direct access (by record number) or RRDS files in addition to the
keyed or KSDS files) unless I was doing something unusual with
sequential files, I suspect I was not alone in this attitude.

Clark Morris

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Conclusions WAS: Re: Can File status checking be dangerous?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-25T13:49:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cd24b574-d2b2-4df4-8814-edc3aa8888fd@wp3g2000pbc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<a1tk7bF8naU1@mid.individual.net> <a281koFdh2U1@mid.individual.net> <a282thFldjU1@mid.individual.net> <bfc7c4c5-9124-424f-bad4-a1d92594c0df@n8g2000pbv.googlegroups.com> <a29c61FaiaU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On May 26, 12:27 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On May 25, 12:43 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> mainframe site in North London many years ago.

You should note that after a WRITE or REWRITE the file buffer becomes
undefined.

    REWRITE xrec
    IF ( file-status = "23" )
        WRITE xrec
    END-IF

_May_ cause a corruption (unless FROM is used) because xrec may no
longer contain the record that you tried to REWRITE.

I learnt this the hard way on ICL 1900 40 years ago. The file system
for ISAM files had overflow areas. It held the records in a nominal
sequence withing the data 'buckets'. If a bucket already had records
'H' and 'L' and one write record 'J' it had to go physically in that
bucket between the two (by shifting 'L'). If there was not room then
it would put a tag between the two and the record into first level
overflow. The tag pointed to where the record really was.

Now we move to the tricky part. It write the record to 1st level goes
back to the bucket to insert the tag, but there is not enough room for
the tag. It can move an existing record to 1st level and then can
write two tags. Where does it keep the displaced record while moving:
- in the program's record buffer.

If it then finds there is not enough room in 1st level it can use 2nd
level overflow. If there is no room there the WRITE fails with a file
status.

Actually the record being written _is_ in the file and the one now in
the record buffer is a completely different record that may have been
in the file for years.

It is unlikely that this would ever be needed by a modern system, but
the record area may be used for data compression, or other purposes
prior to a physical WRITE or REWRITE.

In my systems it is usual to do a READ WITH LOCK so it is always known
whether a WRITE or REWRITE is required.


> And yet I have never worked on a site where it was standard practice.
> (Although I have worked on a lot of sites, I accept that the possible
…[65 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

Your example is different isn't it? It contains inline statements "to

[1] PERFORM 1000-READ-INPUT END-PERFORM

mindless compilers.

>Pete.

No, I think it was the Book of Job.

About learning COBOL, back before PCs I was required to take 2
semesters of COBOL in college (along with other languages and IT type
classes).  Just learning the language does not a programmer make.  I
used to tutor students from Devry where they only learned a language
(usually COBOL but some RPG) and maybe a business class like
accounting and/or economics.  They struggled with the syntax and they
struggled with logic because they hadn't learned those basics.  I
think in today's world it would still be difficult to just learn the
language and then go be a productive programmer.

Regards,
```

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Wed, 23 May 2012 21:18:54 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, I think it was the Book of Job.

:-)

>
> About learning COBOL, back before PCs I was required to take 2
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> language and then go be a productive programmer.

I have never suggested that, Steve.

My comment (in several places here and elsewhere) is that TODAY'S 
PROGRAMMERS can learn all the COBOL they need to know quite easily in a few 
weeks.

(Actually, less than that - I know one case of a guy who was a Java 
programmer who was asked to fix a COBOL problem and had never seen COBOL. He 
did it in two days, and did it correctly.) COBOL is not rocket science and 
is nowhere near as sophisticated as modern OO languages.  People who deal 
happily with those, and have been properly trained in principles of 
programming should not have a problem with COBOL.)

I was asked on one occasion to fix some Pascal (never having learned the 
language). It took me three days.  (If I had never mastered Java, C#, and 
COBOL it would have been much longer...)

If you read the Cretaceous COBOL article you would see the full context of 
this statement. I believe they should sit down with somebody who IS a COBOL 
programmer and WHO HAS KNOWLEDGE OF THE BUSINESS, but my thoughts about this 
are explained in the article.

I agree with you that just learning a language doesn't make anybody a 
programmer, however my point is that someone who IS a programmer can learn 
another language and extend their skill set. (I have done this myself and I 
have seen many others do it...) Modern programmers are taught much more 
about the principles of programming  and much more is known and understood 
about programming than was the case 30 years ago.

Pete.
```

I learned COBOL by reading the Burroughs COBOL manual on the coach
section of the Super Chief in 1962.  I did have the advantage of
having had a 1 - 2 week course in FACT (Fully Automatic Compiling
Technique) on the Honeywell 800.  This language also was English like
and supported segmented programs with paging in from tape with out of
memory segments being flagged by setting bad parity on the descriptor
word for them.  I also learned how to patch RCA 301 COBOL programs and
360 assembler by reading the generated code from DOS COBOL programs. 

At one point they had programming on the management rotation where I
worked with people getting x number of weeks (less than 4 as I recall)
training in COBOL and JCL (the anti-language with negative thinking)
and then being programmer analysts for 1 - 2 years.

operations).

#### ↳ Re: Computerworld Article: The COBOL Brain Drain

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> http://www.computerworld.com/s/article/9227263/The_Cobol_Brain_Drain?taxonomyId=154

Charlie, we already discussed this under  the "Interesting Article" thread 
in this very forum.

I note that you posted there.

Is there some reason you wanted to reopen it under a separate thread?

I made my comments under that thread and some of them were incorporated into 
the article I wrote at:

http://java.dzone.com/articles/cretaceous-cobol-can-spawn

In particular see the section headed "Management sometimes don't get it"

In the month since it was published this article has taken over 7600 hits 
and has created more private mail for me than anything else I have ever 
written anywhere. COBOL guys are not the only ones who are pissed of with 
short sighted managers who really don't understand what's important.

My point is that the "COBOL brain drain" is bullshit. The demise of COBOL 
has nothing to do with programmers retiring.

A modern programmer can acquire all the COBOL they need in a few weeks. 
There are long time COBOL programmers available for work; but outsourcing 
has conditioned management to value this job at $8 an hour...

The problem is with the formulation of  long term strategy, and that is a 
Management problem.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-219d4fa9f2"></a>
## Thread: fujitsu 5.0 standard POST getting value — 12 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards)_

### fujitsu 5.0 standard POST getting value

- **From:** federico <federico.priolo@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-05-24T12:09:22-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<43359bc2-9b96-4831-9e49-ef24f09ff385@e20g2000vbm.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi all, I would like to test a cobol standard for use cgi with fujitsu
cobol. The source is going to write and read from standard input/
output using simple display and accept.

The display statements run fine and I can see into my web server the
html correctly.

After a POST I am using the accept from console for getting the value
but the compiler issue this mistake:

Exception Number  : JMP0209I-U [PID:00000484 TID:00000EC4] CANNOT
ACCEPT/DISPLAY FROM SYSTEM CONSOLE. 'READ' 109. PGM=WEB ADR=0040156C

The cobol source is really simple just it's a console that use display
for put html  and
an standard accept for getting input value.

I know that fujitsu use cgi call to manage a web page and I USE them
but this time I wanted use some of  cobol standard without any
specific compiler features.

Thanks for your advices.

Federico

On May 25, 6:09 pm, federico <federico.pri...@gmail.com> wrote:
> > Operating System ? Web Server ? Source Code ?
>

> accept datavar from console.

In case you missed it the first time:

"""Looking at my version 4 Fujitsu COBOL 4.0 User Guide, Chapter 11
has some notes on Accept/Display and how it relates to SYSIN/SYSOUT
and CONSOLE.

In particular it says that you can't use SYSIN/SYSOUT and CONSOLE in
the same program."""

I first wrote COBOL CGI stuff a couple of decades ago using MicroSoft
COBOL 4.5 on OS/2 with the Netscape (I think) Webserver. I had it
running under DRI's Multiuser-DOS as DOS programs, Windows 3.x and 95
with Xitami server, Unix with Netscape and Linux with Apache.

You can find an early input module on Google groups by searching for
'cgiinput.cbl'.

OS/2 needed a CALL to a system routine as the ACCEPT statement could
not be made to complete. Windows (3.1 and 95) needed a completely
different routine. I did have it working with one server on 3.1 now
lost in the dim past, but shifted to Xitami and this could provide all
the environment and input on a disk file with the output also being
sent by a disk file.

I did have it running on Fujitsu COBOL on Windows with Xitami and
Linux with Xitami or Apache a decade or more ago.

When running on Linux/Apache for a POST it does an 'ACCEPT Request-
Buffer(1:Content-Size)'. Content-Size comes from the environment
variables and the size is vital as otherwise the ACCEPT won't
terminate. This ACCEPT does use a 'FROM CONSOLE' when compiled with MS/
MF COBOL but for Fujitsu it is removed.

Fujitsu/Windows/Apache was not a combination that I tried. Win95/
Xitami worked for me and I moved to all Linux soon after.

> The fujtisu version is listed below
>
…[160 more quoted lines elided]…
> 001280      GOBACK.

```
federico wrote:
>> In case you missed it the first time:
>>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> Federico

Federico,

Fujitsu DOES allow you to ACCEPT from CONSOLE.

I think Richard's point was that you can't use CONSOLE and SYSIN/SYSOUT in 
the same program. So, if you want to accept from console you must display to 
console. If you wish to use SYSIN/SYSOUT you cannot use CONSOLE, and vice 
versa.

However, I think there may be some confusion here between STDIN and STDOUT. 
These are the streams where you might expect to deal with CGI, and using 
them does not preclude you from accepting data via the CONSOLE, as far as I 
know.

None of this is anything I  would be likely to do so I claim no expertise in 
this area.

I will be interested tosee your solution when you have found one.

Ciao,

Pete.
```

<a id="t-ba48bc0825"></a>
## Thread: I'm walking backwards for Xmas... — 15 this month / 16 total

### I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-25T13:22:04+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
As part of the current overhaul of DAL code I am looking at sequential 
reversed processing.

My understanding is that for ISAM files there are 2 ways you can read them 
backwards:

1. START... REVERSED  (where this is supported) (A subsequent READ NEXT gets 
the previous record)

2. READ PREVIOUS (or PREV) as an equivalent of  READ NEXT. (START in the 
usual way (without REVERSED) then READ PREV to go backwards from the START 
position.

As the management of SQL cursors across threads tend to be tedious I don't 
really want to have to use one for reading backwards (I don't have much 
choice for reading forwards...). I can get the previous record in sequence 
with a singleton select.

The following COBOL extract from the generated DAL code shows how:

*> Key index is OK so set host variable from key field...
*> "KOR" means "Key of Reference"...
     move KOR-offset (KOR-x1) to K
     move KOR-length (KOR-x1) to L
     move MOST-data-buffer (K:L) to HV-DynamicParm
     move spaces to SQLBuffer
     string
        'SELECT MAX(['
            delimited by size
        keyname (KOR-x1)
            delimited by space
        ']) FROM [ANYCO1-REC]  WHERE ['
            delimited by size
        keyname (KOR-x1)
            delimited by space
        '] < ? '
            delimited by size
              into SQLBuffer
     end-string
     EXEC SQL
        PREPARE  tmpHandle from :SQLBuffer
     END-EXEC
     EXEC SQL
        EXECUTE tmpHandle  INTO :HV-KEY-WORK USING :HV-DynamicParm
     END-EXEC
     *> zero SQLSTATE just means the EXECUTE was OK... it does NOT mean
     *> that data was actually retrieved.
     if SQLSTATE  NOT = '00000'
        move SQLSTATE to MOST-SQLSTATE
        move SQLMSG   to MOST-SQLMSG
        invoke self 'TranslateFileStatus'
        end-invoke
        move MOST-interface-block to datablock
        exit method
     end-if
     if HV-KEY-WORK (1:L) = low-values
        move "No data found or EOF. " to MOST-SQLMSG
        move '02000' to MOST-SQLSTATE
        invoke self 'TranslateFileStatus'
        end-invoke
        move MOST-interface-block to datablock
        exit method
     end-if
     move HV-KEY-WORK (1:L) to ANYCO1-REC (K:L)
                               MOST-rel-data
     move ANYCO1-REC to  MOST-data-buffer
     move MOST-interface-block to datablock
     INVOKE SELF "ANYCO1MTGetRandom"
            using datablock
     END-INVOKE
     .
 p001-end.
     exit method.

The above code uses the key of the record currently in the data buffer to 
dynamically generate a query that gets the key of the previous record. The 
last three lines then use this key to fetch the record randomly. (It 
actually works very well in practice but it is predicated on the assumption 
that there won't be much demand for reversed processing.)

As Fujitsu supports START... REVERSED, Alistair has used it in his AnyVu 
program which is a kind of  DAL exerciser. It is quite elegant; he can flip 
direction forwards or backwards using a START, and all the coded READ NEXTs 
continue to work without change.

Unfortunately, as the Toolset only implements reversed processing using READ 
PREV the Werewolf version of AnyVu simply doesn't work for reversed 
processing.

I had never seen reversed processing implemented in this way until I saw 
Alistair's code. I like it very much and I'll probably implement support for 
it into the Toolset, but before I do I wanted to get any thoughts and 
observations you may have about reversed sequential processing.

Have you ever implemented reversed sequential processing for indexed files 
in COBOL? (even if you haven't please feel free to comment...)

Why was it necessary?

Do you think there is widespread demand for it? For example, would you NOT 
buy a COBOL compiler that DIDN'T have START... REVERSED? Even if it 
implemented READ... PREV?

Are there other ways of reversed processing you have seen used that are not 
mentioned here?

I'm looking for general feelings, experiences, and opinions, as well as any 
specific instances people care to mention.

Cheers,

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-26T00:50:14+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a29dg8Fk6tU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net> <83rtr7lo342qfifq3m726mchipt9tdjedc@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Fri, 25 May 2012 13:22:04 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I used CICS for many years (and even got to work at Hursley Park, where it 
was invented... :-)) and there is a GET PREVIOUS facility in it. You would 
issue a CICS STARTBR using the key of reference, just as you might issue a 
START for COBOL. Follow it with a READPREV instead of a READNEXT and the key 
of the record retrieved is returned in the RIDFLD option of the Command.

The Toolset in its current incarnation does not run on mainframes so it does 
not handle CICS. (It probably isn't fair to say it doesn't handle CICS 
because the presence of CICS (either Assembler level or Command level) will 
not cause it to crash. Those statements just remain untransformed and so 
will execute exactly as they have always done.)

(It would be quite feasible to use it to create a normalised, optimised, DB2 
database from the existing COBOL copybooks, and generate load programs to 
read the VSAM/KSDSs and write the new database. The generated COBOL for the 
load programs would compile and run on the mainframe without problem. 
However, as the mainframe doesn't support Objects in COBOL, as far as I 
know, there would be no possibility to use any created DAL object layer, and 
I don't think the DAL objects would compile successfully on a mainframe. The 
Load process works OK because the generated code is standard procedural 
COBOL; the DAL is object COBOL.)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb-dnSb0JtIvlCLSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net...
> As part of the current overhaul of DAL code I am looking at sequential 
> reversed processing.
…[112 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I have never done reversed sequential processing exctly, but I have done 
"skip sequential" processing, which skips to a point forward or backward in 
an ISAM file and then reads forward from there.  Depending upon the size of 
the file you could create a new file in reverse order. That is kind of a 
brute force way of doing it, however I have seen some applications where 
random access was too slow.  We used sorting to eliminate it so the reads 
were always forward only and the results were greatly decreased overall run 
time.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-26T01:05:18+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a29echFqe1U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net> <bb-dnSb0JtIvlCLSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net...
…[123 more quoted lines elided]…
> and the results were greatly decreased overall run time.

Thanks Charlie.

I have used skip sequential processing on indexed files but never backwards 
:-)

Fortunately modern RDBMS are VERY good at doing random access and the above 
code executes pretty much as quickly as a Cursor moving forward. (Actually, 
they are both so fast it isn't a problem...In some tests I ran a few years 
ago the cursor was returning 900 records a second and the singleton around 
820. The latest version of SQL Server may have improved on this.) My 
objection to using a cursor for reversed reading is that the DAL object is 
maintaining separate threads and it may already have a forward cursor open 
when someone decides to start a reversed one. Add to this random updates and 
accesses on GET and PUT threads and the whole thing gets a bit messy. (It is 
far from impossible; I just don't want to do it. It seems reasonable to me 
to offer ONE direction of sequential access at any given moment although it 
would be quite feasible to implement both directions and even multiple 
cursors in each direction, all connected to the same application program.)

I have a feeling that reversed processing is not used a lot. I have never 
worked on a site where it was used and I have only one client who uses it. 
(And they don't use it extensively; it is to manage time dependent data 
where they need to show data chronologically and for various reasons they 
don't want to sort it.) But that's why I'm trying to get a feeling from this 
community.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2012-05-25T14:27:50+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jpo4t6$hdo$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<a2855uF3uuU1@mid.individual.net> <bb-dnSb0JtIvlCLSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
In article <bb-dnSb0JtIvlCLSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>,
Charles Hottel <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:

[snip]

>I have never done reversed sequential processing exctly, but I have done 
>"skip sequential" processing, which skips to a point forward or backward in 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>time. 

Ow... the pain, the foulness, the frenzied gyrations needed to comply with 
an 'all ya gotta do'... it was so long ago but I still cringe.

Consider a stripped-down version of a Customer Order File which looked 
something like this:

01  C03452-CUST-ORDFIL.
    05  C03452-KEY.
        10  C03452-CUSTNO PIC X(10) VALUE SPACES.
        10  C03452-ORDDT  PIC 9(06) VALUE ZEROES.
        10  C03452-ORDTM  PIC 9(06) VALUE ZEROES.
        10  C03452-ITMNO  OCCURS 60 PIC X(10)
                                    VALUE SPACES.

... and assuming that a given customer didn't enter more than an order a 
second (and given the systems of the time... they didn't) what would 
result is a dataset containing, for any given customer, the most recent 
order first.

Now... if someone wanted historical data in descending date-time sequence 
there'd be a few ways to do it.  One would be an extractive sort with some 
fields specified ASCENDING and others DESCENDING, eg 

(INCLUDE COND=1,10,CH,EQ,'NYC0010033'),
(SORT FIELDS=(1,10,CH,A,11,12,CH,D)

... to give an historical record of that customer's purchases.  The other 
method - the one that still give shivvers - is to include as part of the 
processing the inelegant kludge of

SUBTRACT WK-C03452-ORDDT FROM 999999 GIVING WK-C03452-ORDDT
SUBTRACT WK-C03452-ORDTM FROM 999999 GIVING WK-C03452-ORDTM

... and write the results out to a temp datatset for processing.

Good thing nobody know what went on back then, eh?

DD
```

 999999

 878787

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I'm walking backwards for Xmas...

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2012-05-26T12:45:57-04:00

## Thread: US CIO/CTO: Idea of Hiring COBOL Coders Laughable — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### US CIO/CTO: Idea of Hiring COBOL Coders Laughable

- **From:** theodp <theodp@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-05-26T11:39:38-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1fd63676-9533-4962-ae11-2bc118f30a36@w24g2000vby.googlegroups.com>`

```
Your tax dollars at work, kids...

Discussing new government technology initiatives at the TechCrunch
Disrupt Conference, Federal CIO Steven VanRoekel quipped, 'I'm
recruiting COBOL developers, any out there?,' sending Federal CTO Todd
Park into fits of laughter (video). Lest anyone think he was serious
about hiring the old fogies, VanRoekel added: 'Trust me, we still have
it in the Federal government, which is quite, quite scary.'

So what are VanRoekel and Park looking for?

Bad a** innovators — the baddest a** of the bad a**es out there,' Park
explained, 'to design, create, and kick a** for America.'
_______

Post/Links at:
http://yro.slashdot.org/story/12/05/26/1658227/us-ciocto-idea-of-hiring-cobol-coders-laughable

Video:
http://techcrunch.com/events/disrupt-ny-2012/sound-bites/?snapid=31018
```

[snip]

If the Federal CIO finds COBOL scary, I find that is cause for concern.
After all, the Federal government participated in the development
of COBOL and, even though times have changed, the need for business
processing hasn't. I would hope that a CIO would be comfortable with
any language suited to a problem domain.

In any case, the big push apparently is for 'Cloud' computing.

< http://www.cio.gov/ >

< http://www.whitehouse.gov/sites/default/files/omb/egov/digital-government/digital-government-strategy.pdf >

### Hiring CoBOL coders laughable

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2012-05-27T08:45:04-06:00

#### ↳ Re: Hiring CoBOL coders laughable

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-28T03:37:18+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a2f01fFnasU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> http://yro.slashdot.org/story/12/05/26/1658227/us-ciocto-idea-of-hiring-cobol-coders-laughable


I enjoyed this video very much. It is good to see some Civil Servants who 
are actually enthusiastic and fired up about what they are doing.

The commitment to open data and accessibility of it is commendable.

I saw at the mashup a few weeks ago this sort of thing in practice. People 
can build quite staggering applications by combining APIs that give access 
to public data. And you certainly don't have to be an IT whizkid to do it.

As for the comment about COBOL at the start, I'm pretty sure it wasn't meant 
to wound anyone. A throwaway line to raise a laugh.

You have to understand that people who are doing the kind of things these 
guys were talking about actually have no conception of how COBOL would work. 
They see it as some arcane archaic language that they are still stuck with 
and it is scary...

Because it is a political platform you can bet that people who support Obama 
will say it was innocent fun; the ones who don't will get as much mileage 
from this "insult" as they can.

As someone with no political axe to grind, and as a sometime COBOL 
programmer, all I can say is that it didn't offend me.

Maybe there will be some COBOL jobs at City Hall out of this...

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Hiring CoBOL coders laughable

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2012-05-27T17:29:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jpto9m$5te$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<pef4s7hbaj14t81irdvrc57cn4in40cinv@4ax.com> <a2f01fFnasU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <a2f01fFnasU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>Howard Brazee wrote:
>>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>The commitment to open data and accessibility of it is commendable.

I would say that it is... and it isn't.  On the one hand I recall a case 
where a local sherrif was on the take, confiscating drugs and guns that 
were seized as evidence and selling them to folks inside his circle; this 
case was cracked by diligent application of open data laws regarding 
evidence logs.

(The sherrif even staged a break-in at his own offices to cover this up... 
if one might imagine anyone taking seriously a break-in with no signs of 
forced entry.)

On the other hand... I do not believe that anyone of moderate sanity would 
demand that use of public health facilities demands that a patient 
forfeits all doctor-patient confidentiality.  (to the simple-minded who 
would pout 'Well, why *not*?' I would answer 'Let's start with the Old 
Soldiers' Home, shall we?' and then work assiduously to find something of 
greater value to occupy my time.)

>
>I saw at the mashup a few weeks ago this sort of thing in practice. People 
>can build quite staggering applications by combining APIs that give access 
>to public data. And you certainly don't have to be an IT whizkid to do it.

You do, however, need to know which data are covered under what laws lest 
you violate them.  There's also an associated tyranny that worries me... 
consider the case of a person involved in divorce proceedings whose spouse 
manages to get hold of telephone logs.  Now add to a spouse who knows 
about ping-tracing and purposefully removes the battery from their 
telephone to prevent such a thing.  Stir in a bit of 'You take the battery 
out of your telephone?  WHAT DO YOU HAVE TO HIDE???' and there is one, 
easily conceived situation where saying 'I want to be left alone' is 
readily turned into 'I am guilty of wanting my whereabouts to be unknown.'

Me, personally, I don't take the battery out of my telephone... I have a 
tin-can with a string and taking myself out of the loop requires only a 
well-tied knot.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Hiring CoBOL coders laughable

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-05-28T13:30:29+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a2g2plFkskU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<pef4s7hbaj14t81irdvrc57cn4in40cinv@4ax.com> <a2f01fFnasU1@mid.individual.net> <jpto9m$5te$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <a2f01fFnasU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[49 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD

All good points, Doc. Personal freedom is a knife edge which we can only 
walk with vigilance.

As for cell phones... with the ongoing deterioration of my eyesight I found 
the one I have just too annoying.

(It rings and you scrabble to find glasses; you need to make a call and the 
poxy little buttons are too small for easy operation, voice dialling is 
notoriously error-prone, and watching the language being murdered by texting 
is something I really get no enjoyment from. Besides, I'm not good at it and 
I don't want to be. I had a girlfriend who could send me 3 more text 
messages while I was still responding to the first one; it was a doomed 
relationship... :-))

Some time ago I took to leaving it in the trunk of my car for possible 
emergency use and eventually the battery went flat and the pre-paid SIM 
expired. (That's another thing I don't think is at all fair; if you buy a 
cell service up front why should it expire...?) Now when I go on a trip 
there is an extra element of spice because I DON'T have a phone to hand. In 
the old days you went to the nearest farm if you broke down and they pulled 
you into town with a tractor; I guess I'm still in that mode... (If there is 
a real emergency I can charge the phone from the in-car charger in a few 
minutes and by law the emergency number has to be enabled whether you have 
paid for the service or not...).

Most of my friends are horrified that I cannot be reached 24/7 (they have 
the same reaction to my refusal to join Facebook; I'm on LinkedIN but I 
joined when it was a business network and before it became social)... 
Gradually, they are coming to accept that I actually enjoy having some 
control over when I am available and I don't need to be "online" 24/7. The 
younger ones see me as "eccentric"; the older ones wish that they could get 
away with it... (when you have family, you really can't...).

I remember with fondness making tin can telephones as a kid, but it all got 
replaced by walkie talkie kits which were sold as toys and had a range of a 
few hundred yards.

The world (and technologies) moves on...

Pete.
```

> Yes, THAT Richard P. Feynmann.

While it sounds like a book I might have written myself, I was surprised to 
find that it was written by one of my heroes... :-)

I'll try and get a copy...

Thanks for the heads up.

I think the "friends" on Facebook is an interesting phenomenon. I wonder if 
it would have the same impact if they were termed "acquaintances" or 
"contacts"? When we see teenagers committing suicide over it, it becomes 
serious. Insecure people can be greatly affected by it.

I remember my father saying to me when I was quite young "Son, never be 
scared of 'they'. What would 'they' think?, what will 'they' do...? make up 
your own mind, be your own man, and do what you know is right."

(He was a very wise man who died when I was 13 years old. My Mother and I 
both adored him.)

It meant that even as a teenager  in the sixties I was never overwhelmed by 
peer pressure.

It takes time (experience) to learn that many people find confidence 
threatening and to temper independence with kindness and consideration, but 
on balance it is more important to be your own person than to be popular.

The two are not mutually exclusive, of course, but attaining both is 
something that takes a lot of work. :-)

Pete.

(ring, ring)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6ac2d7c9-e47e-4b50-922d-24cb61ede362@pr7g2000pbb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<a2qj0tFkicU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Jun 1, 1:08 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> This morning I was sitting in my armchair writing code (COBOL and C#, so
> this post is not completely off topic:-)) when I noticed that the rug near
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> All suggestions received with interest.

It moves under the action of walking on it and pressing down the pile
of the carpet.

The pile will fall one way underfoot, as you step on the run the
friction will be highest between your foot and the top of the pile, as
the pile is pressed down the ends will move the way it falls taking
the rug with it.

When you lift your foot the pressure is released and the rug is held
in its new position by the rest of the area of contact.
```
